Score-based Generative Models (SGMs) have obtained remarkable results to learnand sample probability distributions of image and audio signals(Song and Ermon,, 2019; Nichol and Dhariwal,, 2021; Dhariwal and Nichol,, 2021).They proceed as follows: the data distribution is mapped to a Gaussian whitedistribution by evolving along a Stochastic Differential Equation (SDE), whichprogressively adds noise to the data. The generation is implemented using thetime-reversed SDE, which transforms a Gaussian white noise into a data sample.At each time step, it pushes samples along the gradient of the log probability,also called score function. This score is estimated by leveraging toolsfrom score-matching and deep neural networks(Hyvärinen and Dayan,, 2005; Vincent,, 2011). At sampling time, thecomputational complexity is therefore proportional to the number of time steps,i.e., the number of forward network evaluations. Early SGMs inSong and Ermon, (2019); Song et al., (2021); Ho et al., (2020) used thousands of timesteps, and hence had a limited applicability.

Diffusion models map a Gaussian white distribution into a highlycomplex data distribution. We thus expect that this process will require a large number of timesteps. It then comes as a surprise that recent approaches havedrastically reduced the the number of time steps. This is achieved by optimizing thediscretization schedule or by modifying the original SGM formulation(Kadkhodaie and Simoncelli,, 2020; Jolicoeur-Martineau et al.,, 2021; Liu et al.,, 2022; Zhang and Chen,, 2022; San-Roman et al.,, 2021; Nachmani et al.,, 2021; Song et al.,, 2020; Kong and Ping,, 2021; Ho et al.,, 2020; Luhman and Luhman,, 2021; Salimans and Ho,, 2022; Xiao et al.,, 2021).High-quality score-based generative models have also been improved by cascadingmultiscale image generations (Saharia et al.,, 2021; Ho et al.,, 2022; Dhariwal and Nichol,, 2021) or with subspace decompositions(Jing et al.,, 2022). We show that with an important modification, these approaches provably improve the performance of SGMs.

A key idea is that typical high-dimensional probability distributions comingfrom physics or natural images have complex multiscale properties. They can besimplified by factorizing them as a product of conditional probabilities ofnormalized wavelet coefficients across scales. These conditional probabilitiesare more similar to Gaussian white noise than the original image distribution,and can thus be sampled more efficiently. On the physics side, this observationis rooted in the renormalization group decomposition in statistical physics(Wilson,, 1971), and has been used to estimate physicalenergies from data Marchand et al., (2022). In image processing, it relies on statisticalobservations of wavelet coefficient properties (Wainwright and Simoncelli,, 1999). A Wavelet Score-based GenerativeModel (WSGM) generates normalized wavelet coefficients from coarse to finescales, as illustrated in Figure 1. The conditional probability ofeach set of wavelet coefficients, given coarse scale coefficients, is sampledwith its own (conditional) SGM. The main result is that a normalization ofwavelet coefficients allows fixing the same discretization schedule at allscales. Remarkably, and as opposed to existing algorithms, it implies that thetotal number of sampling iterations per image pixel does not depend on the imagesize.

After reviewing score-based generation models,Section 2 studies the mathematical properties of its timediscretization, with a focus on Gaussian models and multiscale processes.Images and many physical processes are typically non-Gaussian, but do have asingular covariance with long- and short-range correlations. InSection 3, we explain how to factorize these processesinto probability distributions which capture interactions across scales byintroducing orthogonal wavelet transforms. We shall prove that it allows considering SGMswith the same time schedule at all scales, independently of the imagesize. In Section 4, we present numerical results on Gaussiandistributions, the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} physical model at phase transition, and theCelebA-HQ image dataset (Karras et al.,, 2018). The main contributions of the paper are as follows:•A Wavelet Score-based Generative Model (WSGM) which generates conditionalprobabilities of normalized wavelet coefficients, with the same discretizationschedule at all scales. The number of time steps per image pixel does not needto depend upon the image size to reach a fixed error level.•Theorems controlling errors of time discretizations of SGMs, provingaccelerations obtained by scale separation with wavelets. These results areempirically verified by showing that WSGM provides an acceleration for thesynthesis of physical processes at phase transition and natural image datasets.

We study how the regularity of the score ∇log⁡p∇𝑝\nabla\log p affects thediscretization of (5). Assuming that the score is known,i.e., that st=∇log⁡ptsubscript𝑠𝑡∇subscript𝑝𝑡{s}_{t}=\nabla\log p_{t}, we prove that for Gaussianprocesses, the number of time steps to reach a fixed error ε𝜀\varepsilon depends onthe condition number of its covariance. Suppose that the data distribution is a Gaussianp=𝒩​(0,Σ)𝑝𝒩0Σp=\mathcal{N}(0,\Sigma) with covariance matrix ΣΣ\Sigma, in dimension d𝑑d.Let ptsubscript𝑝𝑡p_{t} be the distribution of xtsubscript𝑥𝑡x_{t}.Using (2), we have:∇log⁡pt​(x)=−(Id+(Σ−Id)​e−2​t)−1​x.∇subscript𝑝𝑡𝑥superscriptIdΣIdsuperscripte2𝑡1𝑥\nabla\log p_{t}(x)=-(\operatorname{Id}+(\Sigma-\operatorname{Id})\mathrm{e}^{-2t})^{-1}x.(6)Let p~tsubscript~𝑝𝑡\tilde{p}_{t} be the distribution of x~tsubscript~𝑥𝑡\tilde{x}_{t} obtained bythe time discretization (5).The approximation error between the distribution p~0subscript~𝑝0\tilde{p}_{0}obtained with the time-reversed SDE and the data distribution p𝑝p stems from(i) the mismatch between the distributions of xTsubscript𝑥𝑇x_{T} and x~Tsubscript~𝑥𝑇\tilde{x}_{T}, and(ii) the time discretization.The following theorem relates these two errors to the covariance ΣΣ\Sigma of x𝑥xin the particular case of a uniform time sampling at intervalsδk=δsubscript𝛿𝑘𝛿\delta_{k}=\delta. We normalize the signal energy by imposing that Tr​(Σ)=dTrΣ𝑑{\rm Tr}(\Sigma)=d,and we writeκ𝜅\kappa the condition number of ΣΣ\Sigma, which is the ratio between itslargest and smallest eigenvalues.

This theorem specifies the dependence of the Kullback-Leibler error on thecovariance matrix. It computes an upper bound on the number of time stepsN=T/δ𝑁𝑇𝛿N=T/\delta as a function of the condition number κ𝜅\kappa of ΣΣ\Sigma.As expected, it indicates that the number of time steps increases with thecondition number of the covariance. This theorem is proved in a more general case in Section 10, which includes the case where p𝑝p has a non-zero mean. An exact expansion of the Kullback-Leibler divergence is also given.

For stationary processes of images, the covariance eigenvaluesare given by the power spectrum, which typically decays like |ω|−1superscript𝜔1|{\omega}|^{-1} at afrequency ω𝜔{\omega}. It results that κ𝜅\kappa is proportional to a power of theimage size. Many physical phenomena produce such stationary images with apower spectrum having a power law decay. In these typical cases, the number of time stepsmust increase with the image size. This is indeed what is observed innumerical SGM experiments, as seen in Section 3.

Theorem 1 can beextended to non-Gaussian processes. Thenumber of time steps then depends on the regularity of the score ∇log⁡pt∇subscript𝑝𝑡\nabla\log p_{t}.

The proof of Theorem 2 is postponed toSection 10 and we show that the result can be strengthened byproviding a quantitative upper bound on ‖p−p~0‖TVsubscriptnorm𝑝subscript~𝑝0TV\|p-\tilde{p}_{0}\|_{\mathrm{TV}}.Theorem 2 improves on (De Bortoli et al.,, 2021, Theorem1) by proving explicit bounds exhibiting thedependencies on the regularity constants K𝐾K and M𝑀M of the score and byeliminating an exponential growth term in T𝑇T in the upper bound.Theorem 2 is much more general but not as tight asTheorem 1.

The first error term (11) is due tothe fact that T𝑇T is chosen to be finite.The second error term (12)controls the error depending upon the discretization time step δ𝛿\delta. Sinceptsubscript𝑝𝑡p_{t} is obtained from p𝑝p through a high-dimensional convolution with aGaussian convolution of variance proportional to t𝑡t, the regularity of∇log⁡pt​(x)∇subscript𝑝𝑡𝑥\nabla\log p_{t}(x) typically increases with t𝑡t so‖∇2log⁡pt​(x)‖normsuperscript∇2subscript𝑝𝑡𝑥\|\nabla^{2}\log p_{t}(x)\| and‖∂t∇log⁡pt​(x)‖normsubscript𝑡∇subscript𝑝𝑡𝑥\|\partial_{t}\nabla\log p_{t}(x)\| rather decrease when t𝑡tincrease. This qualitatively explains why a quadratic discretizationschedule with non-uniform time steps δk∝kproportional-tosubscript𝛿𝑘𝑘\delta_{k}\propto k are usually chosen in numericalimplementations of SGMs (Nichol and Dhariwal,, 2021; Song and Ermon,, 2020). Forsimplicity, we focus on the uniform discretization schedule, but our result couldbe adapted to non-uniform time steps with no major difficulties.

If p𝑝p is Gaussian, then the Hessian ∇2log⁡psuperscript∇2𝑝\nabla^{2}\log p is the negative inverse of the covariance matrix. We verify in Section 10 that in this case, the assumptions of Theorem 2 are satisfied. Furthermore, the constants K𝐾K and M𝑀M, and hence the number of discretization steps, are controlled using the condition number of ΣΣ\Sigma. We thus conjecture that non-Gaussian processes with an ill-conditioned covariance matrix will require many discretization steps to have a small error. This will be verified numerically. As we now explain, such processes are ubiquitous in physics and natural image datasets.

Most images have variations on a wide range of scales. They require to usemany time steps to sample using an SGM, because their score is not well-conditioned.This is also true for a wide range of phenomena encountered in physics,biology, or economics (Kolmogorov,, 1962; Mandelbrot,, 1983). We define a multiscaleprocess as a stationary process whose power spectrum has a power lawdecay. The stationarity implies that its covariance is diagonalized in aFourier basis. Its eigenvalues, also called power spectrum, have a power lawdecay defined by:P​(ω)∼(ξη+|ω|η)−1,similar-to𝑃𝜔superscriptsuperscript𝜉𝜂superscript𝜔𝜂1P({\omega})\sim(\xi^{\eta}+|{\omega}|^{\eta})^{-1},(13)where η>0𝜂0\eta>0 and 2​π/ξ2𝜋𝜉2\pi/\xi is the maximum correlation length.Physical processes near phase transitions have such a power-law decay, but it is also the case of many disordered systems such as fluid and gas turbulence.Natural images also typically define stationary processes. Their power spectrum satisfy this property with η=1𝜂1\eta=1and 2​π/ξ≈L2𝜋𝜉𝐿2\pi/\xi\approx L which is the image width.To efficiently synthesize images and more general multiscale signals, we must eliminatethe ill-conditioning properties of the score. This is done by applying a wavelet transform.

The numerical complexity of the SGM algorithm depends on the number of time steps,which itself depends upon the regularity of the score.We show that an important acceleration is obtained byfactorizing the data distribution into normalized wavelet conditionalprobability distributions, which are closer to normal Gaussiandistributions, and so whose score is better-conditioned.

Let x𝑥x be the input signal of width L𝐿L and dimension d=Ln𝑑superscript𝐿𝑛d=L^{n}, with n=2𝑛2n=2 forimages. A conditional wavelet renormalizationfactorizes the distribution p​(x)𝑝𝑥p(x) of signals x𝑥x into conditional probabilities over wavelet coefficients:p​(x)=α​∏j=1Jp¯j​(x¯j|xj)​pJ​(xJ).𝑝𝑥𝛼superscriptsubscriptproduct𝑗1𝐽subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗subscript𝑝𝐽subscript𝑥𝐽\textstyle{p(x)=\alpha\prod_{j=1}^{J}\bar{p}_{j}({\bar{x}}_{j}|x_{j})\,p_{J}(x_{J})~{}.}(16)where α𝛼\alpha (the Jacobian) depends upon all γjsubscript𝛾𝑗\gamma_{j}.

Although p​(x)𝑝𝑥p(x) is typically highly non-Gaussian, the factorization(16) involves distributions that are closer to Gaussians. Thelargest scale distribution pJsubscript𝑝𝐽p_{J} is usually close to a Gaussian when the imagehas independent structures, because xJsubscript𝑥𝐽x_{J} is an averaging of x𝑥x over largedomains of size 2Jsuperscript2𝐽2^{J}. Remarkably, for large classes of signals and images,each conditional probabilitiesp¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\bar{p}_{j}({\bar{x}}_{j}|x_{j}) alsoexhibit Gaussian properties. In images, the wavelet coefficients x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗{\bar{x}}_{j} areusually sparse and thus have highly non-Gaussian distribution; however, it hasbeen observed (Wainwright and Simoncelli,, 1999) that their conditional distributionsp¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\bar{p}_{j}({\bar{x}}_{j}|x_{j}) become much more Gaussian, due to dependencies ofwavelet coefficients across scales. Similarly, in statistical physics,factorization of probabilities of high frequenciesconditioned by lower frequencies have been introduced inWilson, (1983). More recently, normalized waveletfactorizations (16) have been introduced in physics to implementrenormalization group calculations, and model probability distributions withmaximum likelihood estimators near phase transitions (Marchand et al.,, 2022).

Instead of computing a Score-based Generative Model (SGM) of the distributionp​(x)𝑝𝑥p(x), a Wavelet Score-based Generative Model (WSGM) applies an SGM at thecoarsest scale pJ​(xJ)subscript𝑝𝐽subscript𝑥𝐽p_{J}(x_{J}) and then on each conditional distributionp¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\bar{p}_{j}({\bar{x}}_{j}|x_{j}) for j≤J𝑗𝐽j\leq J. It is thus a cascaded SGM, similarlyto Ho et al., (2022); Saharia et al., (2021), but calculated onp¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\bar{p}_{j}({\bar{x}}_{j}|x_{j}) instead of pj​(xj−1|xj)subscript𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript𝑥𝑗1subscript𝑥𝑗p_{j}(x_{j-1}|x_{j}). The normalization ofwavelet coefficients x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗{\bar{x}}_{j} effectively produces a whitening which canconsiderably accelerate the algorithm by reducing the number of time steps. Thisis not possible on xj−1subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j-1} because its covariance is ill-conditioned. It willbe proved for Gaussian processes.

A forward noising process is computed on each x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗{\bar{x}}_{j} for j≤J𝑗𝐽j\leq J and xJsubscript𝑥𝐽x_{J}:d​x¯j,t=−x¯j,t​d​t+2​d​w¯j,t​and​d​xJ,t=−xJ,t​d​t+2​d​wJ,t,dsubscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑡subscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑡d𝑡2dsubscript¯𝑤𝑗𝑡anddsubscript𝑥𝐽𝑡subscript𝑥𝐽𝑡d𝑡2dsubscript𝑤𝐽𝑡\mathrm{d}{\bar{x}}_{j,t}=-{\bar{x}}_{j,t}\,\mathrm{d}t+\sqrt{2}\mathrm{d}\bar{w}_{j,t}~{}~{}\mbox{and}~{}~{}\mathrm{d}x_{J,t}=-x_{J,t}\,\mathrm{d}t+\sqrt{2}\mathrm{d}w_{J,t},(17)where the w¯j,t,wJ,tsubscript¯𝑤𝑗𝑡subscript𝑤𝐽𝑡\bar{w}_{j,t},w_{J,t} are Brownian motions. Since x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j} isnearly white and has Gaussian properties, this diffusion converges much morequickly than if applied directly on x𝑥x. Using (4), we compute ascore function sJ,t​(xJ,t)subscript𝑠𝐽𝑡subscript𝑥𝐽𝑡{{s}}_{J,t}(x_{J,t}) which approximates the score∇log⁡pJ,t​(xJ,t)∇subscript𝑝𝐽𝑡subscript𝑥𝐽𝑡\nabla\log p_{J,t}(x_{J,t}). For each j≤J𝑗𝐽j\leq J we also compute theconditional score s¯j,t​(x¯j,t|xj)subscript¯𝑠𝑗𝑡conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑡subscript𝑥𝑗\bar{{s}}_{j,t}({\bar{x}}_{j,t}|x_{j}) whichapproximates ∇log⁡p¯j,t​(x¯j,t|xj)∇subscript¯𝑝𝑗𝑡conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑡subscript𝑥𝑗\nabla\log\bar{p}_{j,t}({\bar{x}}_{j,t}|x_{j}).

The inverse generative process is computed from coarse to fine scales as follows.At the largest scale 2Jsuperscript2𝐽2^{J}, we samplethe low-dimensional xJsubscript𝑥𝐽x_{J} by time discretizing the inverse SDE. Similarly to(5), the generative process is given by:xJ,tk+1=xJ,tk+δk​{xJ,tk+2​sJ,tk​(xJ,tk)}+2​δk​zJ,k,zJ,k​∼i.i.d.​𝒩​(0,Id).subscript𝑥𝐽subscript𝑡𝑘1subscript𝑥𝐽subscript𝑡𝑘subscript𝛿𝑘subscript𝑥𝐽subscript𝑡𝑘2subscript𝑠𝐽subscript𝑡𝑘subscript𝑥𝐽subscript𝑡𝑘2subscript𝛿𝑘subscript𝑧𝐽𝑘subscript𝑧𝐽𝑘i.i.d.similar-to𝒩0Idx_{J,t_{k+1}}=x_{J,t_{k}}+\delta_{k}\{x_{J,t_{k}}+2{{s}}_{J,t_{k}}(x_{J,t_{k}})\}+\sqrt{2\delta_{k}}z_{J,k},\qquad z_{J,k}\overset{\textup{i.i.d.}}{\sim}{\cal N}(0,\operatorname{Id}).(18)For j𝑗j going from J𝐽J to 111, we then generate the waveletcoefficients x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗{\bar{x}}_{j} conditionally to the previously calculated xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j}, bykeeping the same time discretization schedule at all scales:x¯j,tk+1=x¯j,tk+δk​{x¯j,tk+2​s¯j,tk​(x¯j,tk|xj)}+2​δk​zj,k,zj,k​∼i.i.d.​𝒩​(0,Id).subscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑡𝑘1subscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑡𝑘subscript𝛿𝑘subscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑡𝑘2subscript¯𝑠𝑗subscript𝑡𝑘conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑡𝑘subscript𝑥𝑗2subscript𝛿𝑘subscript𝑧𝑗𝑘subscript𝑧𝑗𝑘i.i.d.similar-to𝒩0Id{\bar{x}}_{j,t_{k+1}}={\bar{x}}_{j,t_{k}}+\delta_{k}\{{\bar{x}}_{j,t_{k}}+2\bar{{s}}_{j,t_{k}}({\bar{x}}_{j,t_{k}}|x_{j})\}+\sqrt{2\delta_{k}}\,z_{j,k},\qquad z_{j,k}\overset{\textup{i.i.d.}}{\sim}{\cal N}(0,\operatorname{Id}).(19)The inverse wavelet transform then approximately computesa sample of xj−1subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j-1} from (x¯j,0,xj)subscript¯𝑥𝑗0subscript𝑥𝑗({\bar{x}}_{j,0},x_{j}):x~j−1=γj​G⊤​xj+γj​G¯⊤​x¯j,0.subscript~𝑥𝑗1subscript𝛾𝑗superscript𝐺topsubscript𝑥𝑗subscript𝛾𝑗superscript¯𝐺topsubscript¯𝑥𝑗0\tilde{x}_{j-1}=\gamma_{j}\,G^{\top}x_{j}+\gamma_{j}\,{\bar{G}}^{\top}{\bar{x}}_{j,0}.(20)The generative process is illustrated in Figure 1 and its pseudocode is given in Section 7, seeAlgorithm 1. The appendix also verifies that if x𝑥x is of size d𝑑d then its numerical complexity is O​(N​d)𝑂𝑁𝑑O(Nd) where N𝑁N is the number of time steps, which is the same at each scale. For multiscale processes, we shall see that the number of time steps N𝑁N does not depend upon d𝑑d to reach a fixed error measured with a KL divergence.

The use of wavelets in multiscale architectures is often motivated by the spectral bias phenomenon Rahaman et al., (2019): Huang et al., (2017) uses a wavelet CNN for super-resolution, Gal et al., (2021) incorporates spectral information by training GANs in the wavelet domain. Closer in spirit to our work, Yu et al., (2020) introduces Wavelet Flow, a normalizing flow with a cascade of layers generating wavelet coefficients conditionally on lower-scales, then aggregating them with an inverse wavelet transform. This method yields training time acceleration and high-resolution (1024x1024) generation.

WSGM is closely related to other cascading diffusion algorithms, such as the onesintroduced in Ho et al., (2022); Saharia et al., (2021); Dhariwal and Nichol, (2021). Themain difference lies in that earlier works on cascaded SGMs do not model thewavelet coefficients {x¯j}j=1Jsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑗1𝐽\{\bar{x}_{j}\}_{j=1}^{J} but the low-frequencycoefficients {xj}j=1Jsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑥𝑗𝑗1𝐽\{x_{j}\}_{j=1}^{J}. As a result, cascaded models do not exploit thewhitening properties of the wavelet transform. Wealso point out the recent work of Jing et al., (2022) which, while not usingthe cascading framework, drop subspaces from the noising process at differenttimes. This allows using only one SDE to sample approximately from the datadistribution. However, the reconstruction is still computed with respect to{xj}j=1Jsuperscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑥𝑗𝑗1𝐽\{x_{j}\}_{j=1}^{J} instead of the wavelet coefficients.

Finally, we highlight that our work could be combined with other accelerationtechniques such as the ones ofJolicoeur-Martineau et al., (2021); Liu et al., (2022); Zhang and Chen, (2022); San-Roman et al., (2021); Nachmani et al., (2021); Song et al., (2020); Ho et al., (2020); Kong and Ping, (2021); Luhman and Luhman, (2021); Salimans and Ho, (2022); Xiao et al., (2021)in order to improve the empirical results of WSGM.

We now illustrate Theorem 1 and the effectiveness of WSGM on Gaussian multiscale processes. We use thewhitening properties of the wavelet transform to show that the timecomplexity required in order to reach a given error is linear in the image dimension.

The following result proves that the normalization of wavelet coefficientsperforms a preconditioning of the covariance, whose eigenvalues then remain of theorder of 111. This is a consequence of a theorem proved by Meyer, (1992) onthe representation of classes of singular operators in wavelet bases, seeSection 9. As a result, the number of iterations T/δ𝑇𝛿T/\delta required to reach an error ε𝜀\varepsilon is independent of the dimension.

To prove this result, we show that the conditioning number of the covariance matrix of the renormalized wavelet coefficients does not depend on the dimension using (Meyer,, 1992). We conclude upon combining this result, the cascading property of the Kullback-Leibler divergence and an extension of Theorem 1 to the setting with non-zero mean. The detailed proof is postponed to Section 11.

We illustrate Theorem 3 on a Gaussianfield x𝑥x, with power spectrum P𝑃P as (13). In Figure 2, we display the sup-norm between P𝑃P andthe power spectrum P^^𝑃\hat{P} of the samples obtained using either vanilla SGM or WSGMwith uniform stepsize δk=δsubscript𝛿𝑘𝛿\delta_{k}=\delta. In the case of vanilla SGM, the number N​(ε)𝑁𝜀N(\varepsilon) of time steps needed to reach a small error ‖P−P^‖=εnorm𝑃^𝑃𝜀\|P-\hat{P}\|=\varepsilon increases with the size of the image L𝐿L Figure 2, right). Equation (9) suggests that N​(ε)𝑁𝜀N(\varepsilon) scales like a power of the conditioning number κ𝜅\kappa of ΣΣ\Sigma, which, for multiscale gaussian processes, is κ∼Lηsimilar-to𝜅superscript𝐿𝜂\kappa\sim L^{\eta}. In the WSGM case, we sample conditional distributions p¯jsubscript¯𝑝𝑗\bar{p}_{j} of wavelets x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j} given low-frequencies xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j}. At scale j𝑗j, the conditioning numbers κ¯jsubscript¯𝜅𝑗\bar{\kappa}_{j} of the conditional covariance become dimension-independent (Section 9), removing the dependency of N​(ε)𝑁𝜀N(\varepsilon) on the image size L𝐿L as suggested by (21).

For multiscale Gaussian processes, we proved that with WSGMs, the number of time steps N​(ε)𝑁𝜀N(\varepsilon) to reach a fixed error ε𝜀\varepsilon does not depend on the signal size, as opposed to SGMs. This sectionshows that this result applies to non-Gaussian multiscale processes. We considera physical process near a phase transition and images from the CelebA-HQ database (Karras et al.,, 2018).

Gaussian stationary processes are maximum entropy processes conditioned by second order moments defined by a circulant matrix. More complex physical processes are modeled by imposing a constraint on their marginal distribution, with a so-called scalar potential.The marginal distribution of x𝑥x is the probability distribution of x​(u)𝑥𝑢x(u), which does not depend upon u𝑢u if x𝑥x is stationary. Maximum entropy processes conditioned by second order moments and marginal distributions have a probability density which is a Gibbs distributionp​(x)=Z−1​e−E​(x)𝑝𝑥superscript𝑍1superscripte𝐸𝑥p(x)=Z^{-1}\,\mathrm{e}^{-E(x)} with:E​(x)=12​x⊤​C​x+∑uV​(x​(u)),𝐸𝑥12superscript𝑥top𝐶𝑥subscript𝑢𝑉𝑥𝑢\textstyle{E(x)=\frac{1}{2}x^{\top}Cx+\sum_{u}V(x(u))~{}~{},}(22)where C𝐶C is a circulant matrix and V:ℝ→ℝ:𝑉→ℝℝV\colon\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R} is a scalar potential.Section 13 explains how to parametrize V𝑉V as a linear combination of a family of fixed elementary functions. The φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} model is a particular example where C=Δ𝐶ΔC=\Delta is a Laplacian and V𝑉V is a fourth order polynomial, adjusted in order to imposes that x​(u)=±1𝑥𝑢plus-or-minus1x(u)=\pm 1 with high probability. For so-called critical values of these parameters, the resulting process becomes multiscale with long range interactions and a power law spectrum, seeFigure 3-(c).

We train SGMs and WSGMs on critical φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} processes of different sizes; for the score model sθsubscript𝑠𝜃{{s}}_{\theta}, we use a simple linear parametrization detailed in Section 13.2. To evaluate the quality of the generated samples, it is sufficient to verify thatthese samples have the same second order moment and marginals as φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4}. We define the error metric as the sum of the L2superscriptL2\mathrm{L}^{2} error on the power spectrum and the total-variation distance between marginal distributions. Figure 3-(a) shows the decay of this error as a function of the number of time steps used in an SGM andWSGM with a uniform discretization. With vanilla SGM, the loss has a strong dependency in L𝐿L, but becomes almost independent of L𝐿L for WSGM.This empirically verifies the claim that an ill-conditioned covariance matrix leads to slow sampling of SGM, and that WSGM is unaffected by this issue by working with the conditional distributions of normalized wavelet coefficients.

Images are highly non-Gaussian multiscale processes whose power spectrum has a power law decay. We now show that WSGM also provides an acceleration over SGM in this case, by being independent of the image size.

We focus on the CelebA-HQ dataset (Liu et al.,, 2015) at a 128×128128128128\times 128 resolution. Though non-stationary, its power spectrum still has a power law decay, as shown in Figure 4. We compare SGM Ho et al., (2020) samples at the 128×128128128128\times 128 resolution with WSGM samples which start from the 32×32323232\times 32 resolution. Though smaller, the 32×32323232\times 32 resolution still suffers from a power law decay of its spectrum over several orders of magnitude. The reason why we limit this coarsest resolution is because border effects become dominant at lower image sizes. To simplify the handling of border conditions, we use Haar wavelets.

Following Nichol and Dhariwal, (2021), the global scores sθ​(x)subscript𝑠𝜃𝑥{s}_{\theta}(x) are parametrized by a neural network with a UNet architecture. It has 3 residual blocks at each scale, and includes multi-head attention layers at lower scales. The conditional scores sθ​(x¯j|xj)subscript𝑠𝜃conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗{s}_{\theta}(\bar{x}_{j}|x_{j}) are parametrized in the same way, and the conditioning on the low frequencies xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} is done with a simple input concatenation along channels (Nichol and Dhariwal,, 2021; Saharia et al.,, 2021). The details of the architecture are in Section 14. We use a uniform discretization of the backward SDE to stay in the setting of Theorem 2.

The generation results are given in Figure 4. With the same computational budget of 161616 discretizations steps at the largest scale (iterations at smaller scales having a negligible cost due to the exponential decrease in image size), WSGM achieves a much better perceptual generation quality. Notably, SGM generates noisy images due to discretization errors. This is confirmed quantitatively with the Fréchet Inception Distance (FID) (Heusel et al.,, 2017). The FID of the WSGM generations decreases with the number of steps, until it plateaus. This plateau is reached with at least 2 orders of magnitude less steps for WSGM than SGM. This number of steps is also independent of the image size for WSGM, thus confirming the intuition given in the Gaussian case by Theorem 1-3. Our results confirm that vanilla SGM on a wide range of multiscale processes, including natural images, suffers from ill-conditioning, in the sense that the number of discretization steps grows with the image size. WSGM, on the contrary, leads to uniform discretization schemes whose number of steps at each scale does not depend on the image size.

We also stress that there exists many techniques (Kadkhodaie and Simoncelli,, 2020; Jolicoeur-Martineau et al.,, 2021; Liu et al.,, 2022; Zhang and Chen,, 2022; San-Roman et al.,, 2021; Nachmani et al.,, 2021; Song et al.,, 2020; Kong and Ping,, 2021; Ho et al.,, 2020; Luhman and Luhman,, 2021; Salimans and Ho,, 2022; Xiao et al.,, 2021) to accelerate the sampling of vanilla SGMs, with sometimes better FID-time complexity tradeoff curves. Notably, the FID plateaus at a relatively high value of 20 because the coarsest resolution 32×32323232\times 32 is still ill-conditioned and requires thousands of steps with a non-uniform discretization schedule to achieve FIDs less than 10 with vanilla SGM Nichol and Dhariwal, (2021). Such improvements (including proper handling of border conditions) are beyond of the scope of this paper. The contribution of WSGM is rather to show the reason behind this sampling inefficiency and mathematically prove in the Gaussian setting that wavelet decompositions of the probability distribution allow solving this problem. We believe that extending this theoretical result to a wider class of non-Gaussian multiscale processes and combining WSGM with other sampling accelerations are interesting avenues for future work.

This paper introduces a Wavelet Score-based Generative Model (WSGM)which applies an SGM to normalized wavelet coefficients conditioned by lower frequencies.We prove that the number ofstepsizes in SGMs is controlled by the regularity of the score of the targetdistribution. For multiscale processes such as images, it requires a considerable numberof time steps to achieve a good accuracy, which increases quickly with the image size.We show that a WSGM eliminates ill-conditioning issues by normalizingwavelet coefficients. As a result, the number of stepsizes in WSGM does not increasewith the image dimension.We illustrated our results on Gaussiandistributions, physical processes and image datasets.

One of the main limitations of WSGM is that it is limited to multiscale processes for which the conditional wavelet probabilities are close to white Gaussian distributions. A promising direction for future work is to combine WSGM with other acceleration techniques such as adaptive time discretizations to handle such cases.In another direction, one could strengthen the theoreticalstudy of SGM and extend our results beyond the Gaussian setting, inorder to fully describe SGM on physical processes which can be seen asperturbations of Gaussian distributions.

In Algorithm 1, we provide the pseudo code for WSGM. Notice that the training of score models at each scale can be done in parallel, while the sampling is done sequentially one scale after the next. The sequential nature of sampling is the price to pay for the conditional decomposition, as we model only the conditional scores ∇x¯jlog⁡p¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript∇subscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\nabla_{\bar{x}_{j}}\log\bar{p}_{j}(\bar{x}_{j}|x_{j}). Generating all scales simultaneously would require learning the so-called free energy gradient ∇xjlog⁡p¯j​(x¯j|xj)subscript∇subscript𝑥𝑗subscript¯𝑝𝑗conditionalsubscript¯𝑥𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗\nabla_{x_{j}}\log\bar{p}_{j}(\bar{x}_{j}|x_{j}). It is also required for likelihood computation. However, the conditional decomposition of WSGM allows not learning this free energy gradient and possibly reduces score estimation errors.

This section introduces properties of orthogonal wavelet bases as specified by the operators G𝐺G and G¯¯𝐺\bar{G}. In this section, we drop the normalizing factors γjsubscript𝛾𝑗\gamma_{j} for clarity.

Let x0subscript𝑥0x_{0} be a signal. The index u𝑢u in x0​(u)subscript𝑥0𝑢x_{0}(u) belongs to an n𝑛n-dimensional grid of linear size L𝐿L and hence with Lnsuperscript𝐿𝑛L^{n} sites, with n=2𝑛2n=2 for images.

Let us denote xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} the coarse-grained version of x0subscript𝑥0x_{0} at a scale 2jsuperscript2𝑗2^{j} defined over a coarser grid with intervals 2jsuperscript2𝑗2^{j} and hence (2−j​L)nsuperscriptsuperscript2𝑗𝐿𝑛(2^{-j}L)^{n} sites. The coarser signal xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} is iteratively computed from xj−1subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j-1} by applying a coarse-graining operator, which acts as a scaling filter G𝐺G which eliminates high frequencies and subsamplesthe grid Mallat, (1989):(G​xj−1)​(u)=∑u′xj−1​(u′)​G​(2​u−u′).𝐺subscript𝑥𝑗1𝑢subscriptsuperscript𝑢′subscript𝑥𝑗1superscript𝑢′𝐺2𝑢superscript𝑢′(Gx_{j-1})(u)=\sum_{u^{\prime}}x_{j-1}(u^{\prime})\,G(2u-u^{\prime})\,\,.(23)The index u𝑢u on the left-hand side runs on the coarser grid, whereas u′superscript𝑢′u^{\prime} runs on the finer one.

The degrees of freedom of xj−1subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j-1} that are not in xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} are encoded in orthogonal wavelet coefficients x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j}.The representation (xj,x¯j)subscript𝑥𝑗subscript¯𝑥𝑗(x_{j},\bar{x}_{j})is an orthogonal change of basis calculated from xj−1subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j-1}. The coarse signalxjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} is calculated in (23) with a low-pass scaling filter G𝐺G and a subsampling. In dimension n𝑛n,the wavelet coefficients x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j} have 2n−1superscript2𝑛12^{n}-1 channels computed with a convolution and subsampling operator G¯¯𝐺\bar{G}. We thus have:xj=G​xj−1​and​x¯j=G¯​xj−1.subscript𝑥𝑗𝐺subscript𝑥𝑗1andsubscript¯𝑥𝑗¯𝐺subscript𝑥𝑗1x_{j}=G\,x_{j-1}~{}~{}\mbox{and}~{}~{}\bar{x}_{j}=\bar{G}\,x_{j-1}.(24)The wavelet filterG¯¯𝐺\bar{G} computes 2n−1superscript2𝑛12^{n}-1 wavelet coefficients x¯j​(u,k)subscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑢𝑘\bar{x}_{j}(u,k) indexed by 1≤k≤2n−11𝑘superscript2𝑛11\leq k\leq 2^{n}-1,with separable high-pass filters G¯k​(u)subscript¯𝐺𝑘𝑢\bar{G}_{k}(u):x¯j​(u,k)=∑u′xj−1​(u′)​G¯k​(2​u−u′).subscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑢𝑘subscriptsuperscript𝑢′subscript𝑥𝑗1superscript𝑢′subscript¯𝐺𝑘2𝑢superscript𝑢′\bar{x}_{j}(u,k)=\sum_{u^{\prime}}x_{j-1}(u^{\prime})\,\bar{G}_{k}(2u-u^{\prime}).

As an example, the Haar wavelet leads to a block averaging filter G𝐺G:xj​(u)=xj−1​(2​u)+xj−1​(2​u+1)2.subscript𝑥𝑗𝑢subscript𝑥𝑗12𝑢subscript𝑥𝑗12𝑢12x_{j}(u)=\frac{x_{j-1}(2u)+x_{j-1}(2u+1)}{\sqrt{2}}.In n=1𝑛1n=1 dimension, there is a single wavelet channel in x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j}. The corresponding wavelet filter G¯¯𝐺\bar{G} computes the wavelet coefficients with normalized increments:x¯j​(u)=xj−1​(2​u)−xj−1​(2​u+1)2.subscript¯𝑥𝑗𝑢subscript𝑥𝑗12𝑢subscript𝑥𝑗12𝑢12\bar{x}_{j}(u)=\frac{x_{j-1}(2u)-x_{j-1}(2u+1)}{\sqrt{2}}.(25)If n=2𝑛2n=2, then there are 333 channels as illustrated in Figure 1.

The wavelet orthonormal filters G𝐺G and G¯¯𝐺\bar{G} definea unitary transformation, which satisfies:G¯​G⊤=G​G¯⊤=0​and​G⊤​G+G¯T​G¯=Id,¯𝐺superscript𝐺top𝐺superscript¯𝐺top0andsuperscript𝐺top𝐺superscript¯𝐺𝑇¯𝐺Id\bar{G}G^{\top}=G\bar{G}^{\top}=0~{}~{}\mbox{and}~{}~{}G^{\top}G+\bar{G}^{T}\bar{G}=\mathrm{Id}~{},(27)where IdId\mathrm{Id} is the identity. This appendix relates the fast discrete wavelet transform to decomposition offinite energy functions in orthonormal bases of 𝐋𝟐​([0,1]n)superscript𝐋2superscript01𝑛{\bf L^{2}}([0,1]^{n}). We shall thenprove that the covariance of normalized wavelet coefficients is a well-conditioned matrix, independently from scales. This is a central result to prove Theorem 3. The results of this appendix are based onthe multiresolution theory Mallat, (1999) and the representation ofsingular operators in wavelet orthonormal bases Meyer, (1992).

The multiresolution wavelet theory Mallat, (1999); Meyer, (1992) provesthat the coefficients of x¯wsuperscript¯𝑥𝑤{\bar{x}}^{w} can also be written as the decompositioncoefficients of a finite energy function, in a wavelet orthonormal basis of thespace 𝐋𝟐​(ℝn)superscript𝐋2superscriptℝ𝑛{\bf L^{2}}(\mathbb{R}^{n}) of finite energy functions. The following lemma proves that the covariance matrixΣ¯¯Σ\bar{\Sigma} of the normalized wavelet coefficients x¯¯𝑥{\bar{x}} of x𝑥x is wellconditioned, with a condition number which does not depend upon L𝐿L. It relies on an equivalence between Sobolev norms and weighted norms in a wavelet orthonormal basis.

In this section, we first present the continuous-time framework in a Gaussiansetting in Section 10.1. The general outline of the proof ofTheorem 1 is presented inSection 10.2. Technical lemmas are gathered inSection 10.3. The proof of Theorem 2 ispresented in Section 10.4.

In what follows we present the Gaussian setting used in the proof ofTheorem 1. We will use the following result.

We now prove Theorem 4.

We are going to make use of the following lemma which is a direct consequence ofthe Euler-MacLaurin formula.

We are now ready to prove the following results.

Finally, we have the following proposition.

Finally, we have the following result.

We are now ready to prove Theorem 2.

The beginning of the proof is similar to the one of (De Bortoli et al.,, 2021, Theorem1). We now check that the assumption of Theorem 2 are satisfied in a Gaussian setting.

Therefore, we get the following simplified result in the Gaussian setting.

We now give some details on the experiments in Section 3.2 (Figure 2). We use the exact formulas for the Stein score of ptsubscript𝑝𝑡p_{t} in this case: if x0∼𝒩​(M,Σ)similar-tosubscript𝑥0𝒩𝑀Σx_{0}\sim\mathcal{N}(M,\Sigma), then xt∼N​(Mt,Σt)similar-tosubscript𝑥𝑡𝑁subscript𝑀𝑡subscriptΣ𝑡x_{t}\sim N(M_{t},\Sigma_{t}) with Mt=e−t​Msubscript𝑀𝑡superscript𝑒𝑡𝑀M_{t}=e^{-t}M andΣt=e−2​t​Σ+(1−e−2​t)​Id.subscriptΣ𝑡superscripte2𝑡Σ1superscripte2𝑡Id\Sigma_{t}=\mathrm{e}^{-2t}\Sigma+(1-\mathrm{e}^{-2t})\operatorname{Id}.Under an ideal situation where there is no score error, the discretization of the (backward) generative process is given by equation (105):xk+1=((1+δ)​Id−2​δ​ΣT−k​δ−1)​xk+2​δ​ΣT−k​δ−1​MT−k​δ+2​δ​zk+1,subscript𝑥𝑘11𝛿Id2𝛿superscriptsubscriptΣ𝑇𝑘𝛿1subscript𝑥𝑘2𝛿superscriptsubscriptΣ𝑇𝑘𝛿1subscript𝑀𝑇𝑘𝛿2𝛿subscript𝑧𝑘1x_{k+1}=((1+\delta)\operatorname{Id}-2\delta\Sigma_{T-k\delta}^{-1})x_{k}+2\delta\Sigma_{T-k\delta}^{-1}M_{T-k\delta}+\sqrt{2\delta}z_{k+1}\;,(428)where δ𝛿\delta is the uniform step size and zksubscript𝑧𝑘z_{k} are iid white Gaussian random variables. For the SGM case, M=0𝑀0M=0. The starting step of this discretization is itself x0∼𝒩​(0,Id)similar-tosubscript𝑥0𝒩0Idx_{0}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,\operatorname{Id}). From this formula, the covariance matrix Σ^ksubscript^Σ𝑘\hat{\Sigma}_{k} of xksubscript𝑥𝑘x_{k} satisfies the recursion (82):Σ^k+1=((1+δ)​Id−2​δ​ΣT−k​δ−1)​Σ^k​((1+δ)​Id−2​δ​ΣT−k​δ−1)+2​δ​Id,subscript^Σ𝑘11𝛿Id2𝛿superscriptsubscriptΣ𝑇𝑘𝛿1subscript^Σ𝑘1𝛿Id2𝛿superscriptsubscriptΣ𝑇𝑘𝛿12𝛿Id\hat{\Sigma}_{k+1}=((1+\delta)\operatorname{Id}-2\delta\Sigma_{T-k\delta}^{-1})\hat{\Sigma}_{k}((1+\delta)\operatorname{Id}-2\delta\Sigma_{T-k\delta}^{-1})+2\delta\operatorname{Id}\;,(429)from which we can exactly compute Σ^ksubscript^Σ𝑘\hat{\Sigma}_{k} for very k𝑘k, and especially for k=N=T/δ𝑘𝑁𝑇𝛿k=N=T/\delta, as a function of ΣΣ\Sigma, the final time T𝑇T, and the step size δ𝛿\delta. In all our experiments, we choose stationary processes: their covariance ΣΣ\Sigma is diagonal in a Fourier basis, with eigenvalues (power spectrum) noted P^ksubscript^𝑃𝑘\hat{P}_{k}. All the xksubscript𝑥𝑘x_{k} remain stationary so Σ^ksubscript^Σ𝑘\hat{\Sigma}_{k} is still diagonal in a Fourier basis, with power spectrum noted P^ksubscript^𝑃𝑘\hat{P}_{k}. The error displayed in the left panel of Figure 2 is:‖P^N−P‖=maxω⁡|P^N​(ω)−P​(ω)|/maxω⁡|P​(ω)|,normsubscript^𝑃𝑁𝑃subscript𝜔subscript^𝑃𝑁𝜔𝑃𝜔subscript𝜔𝑃𝜔\|\hat{P}_{N}-P\|=\max_{\omega}|\hat{P}_{N}(\omega)-P(\omega)|/\max_{\omega}|P(\omega)|,normalized by the operator norm of ΣΣ\Sigma.

The illustration in the middle panel of Figure 2, for WSGM, is done for simplicity only at one scale (ie, at j=1𝑗1j=1 in Algorithm 1): instead of stacking the full cascade of conditional distributions for all j=J,…,1𝑗𝐽…1j=J,\dotsc,1, we use the true low-frequencies xj,0=x1subscript𝑥𝑗0subscript𝑥1x_{j,0}=x_{1}. Here, we use Daubechies-4 wavelets. We sample x¯j,0subscript¯𝑥𝑗0{\bar{x}}_{j,0} using the Euler-Maruyama recursion (105)-(82) for the conditional distribution. We recall that in the Gaussian case, x¯1subscript¯𝑥1\bar{x}_{1} and x1subscript𝑥1x_{1} are jointly Gaussian. The conditional distribution of x¯1subscript¯𝑥1\bar{x}_{1} given x1subscript𝑥1x_{1} is known to be 𝒩​(A​x1,Γ)𝒩𝐴subscript𝑥1Γ\mathcal{N}(Ax_{1},\Gamma), where:A=−Cov​(x¯1,x1)​Var​(x1)−1,𝐴Covsubscript¯𝑥1subscript𝑥1Varsuperscriptsubscript𝑥11\displaystyle A=-\mathrm{Cov}(\bar{x}_{1},x_{1})\mathrm{Var}(x_{1})^{-1},Γ=Var​(x¯1)−Cov​(x¯1,x1)​Var​(x1)−1​Cov​(x¯1,x1)⊤.ΓVarsubscript¯𝑥1Covsubscript¯𝑥1subscript𝑥1Varsuperscriptsubscript𝑥11Covsuperscriptsubscript¯𝑥1subscript𝑥1top\displaystyle\Gamma=\mathrm{Var}(\bar{x}_{1})-\mathrm{Cov}(\bar{x}_{1},x_{1})\mathrm{Var}(x_{1})^{-1}\mathrm{Cov}(\bar{x}_{1},x_{1})^{\top}.(430)We solve the recursion (82) with a step size δ𝛿\delta and N=T/δ𝑁𝑇𝛿N=T/\delta steps; the sampled conditional wavelet coefficients x¯j,0subscript¯𝑥𝑗0{\bar{x}}_{j,0} have conditional distribution noted 𝒩​(A^N​x,Γ^N)𝒩subscript^𝐴𝑁𝑥subscript^Γ𝑁\mathcal{N}(\hat{A}_{N}x,\hat{\Gamma}_{N}). The full covariance of (x~j,0,x¯j,0)subscript~𝑥𝑗0subscript¯𝑥𝑗0(\tilde{x}_{j,0},{\bar{x}}_{j,0}), written in the basis given by the high/low frequencies, is now given byΣ^N=[Γ^NCov​(x1,x¯1)​A^N⊤A^N​Cov​(x1,x¯1)⊤Cov​(x1,x1)].subscript^Σ𝑁matrixsubscript^Γ𝑁Covsubscript𝑥1subscript¯𝑥1superscriptsubscript^𝐴𝑁topsubscript^𝐴𝑁Covsuperscriptsubscript𝑥1subscript¯𝑥1topCovsubscript𝑥1subscript𝑥1\hat{\Sigma}_{N}=\begin{bmatrix}\hat{\Gamma}_{N}&\mathrm{Cov}(x_{1},\bar{x}_{1})\hat{A}_{N}^{\top}\\\hat{A}_{N}\mathrm{Cov}(x_{1},\bar{x}_{1})^{\top}&\mathrm{Cov}(x_{1},x_{1})\end{bmatrix}.Figure 2, middle panel compares the eigenvalues (power spectrum) of these covariances, as a function of δ𝛿\delta, with the ones of ΣΣ\Sigma.

The right panel of Figure 2 gives the smallest N𝑁N needed to reach ‖P^N−P‖=0.1normsubscript^𝑃𝑁𝑃0.1\|\hat{P}_{N}-P\|=0.1 in both cases (SGM and WSGM), based on a power law extrapolation of the curves N↦P^Nmaps-to𝑁subscript^𝑃𝑁N\mapsto\hat{P}_{N}.

In this section, we develop and precise the results in Section 4.1.

The macroscopic energy of non-Gaussian distributions can be specified as shown in (22),where K𝐾K is a coupling matrix and V𝑉V is non-quadratic potential. The φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4}-model over the L×L𝐿𝐿L\times L periodic grid is the special case defined by C=Δ𝐶ΔC=\Delta (the two-dimensional discrete Laplacian) and V𝑉V is a quartic potential:E​(x)=β2​∑|u−v|=1(x​(u)−x​(v))2+∑u(x​(u)2−1)2.𝐸𝑥𝛽2subscript𝑢𝑣1superscript𝑥𝑢𝑥𝑣2subscript𝑢superscript𝑥superscript𝑢212\textstyle{E(x)=\frac{\beta}{2}\sum_{|u-v|=1}(x(u)-x(v))^{2}+\sum_{u}(x(u)^{2}-1)^{2}.}(431)Here, β𝛽\beta is a parameter proportional to an inverse temperature.

In physics, the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} model is a typical example of second-order phase transitions: the quadratic part reduces spatial fluctuations, and V𝑉V favors configurations whose entries remain close to ±1plus-or-minus1\pm 1 (in physics, this is often called a double-well potential). In the thermodynamic limit L→∞→𝐿L\to\infty, both term compete according to the value of β𝛽\beta.•For β≪1much-less-than𝛽1\beta\ll 1, the quadratic term becomes negligible and the marginals of the field become independent; this is the disordered state.•For β≫1much-greater-than𝛽1\beta\gg 1, the quadratic term favors configuration which are spatially smooth and the potential term drives the values towards ±1plus-or-minus1\pm 1, resulting in an ordered state, where all values of the field are simultaneously close to either +11+1 or to −11-1.

A phase transition occurs between these two regimes at a critical temperature βc∼0.68similar-tosubscript𝛽𝑐0.68\beta_{c}\sim 0.68 Milchev et al., (1986); Kaupuvzs et al., (2016). At this point, the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} field display very long-range correlations and an ill-conditioned Hessian ∇2log⁡psuperscript∇2𝑝\nabla^{2}\log p. The sampling of φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} at this critical point becomes very difficult. This “critical slowing down” phenomenon is why, from a machine learning point of view, the critical φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} field is an excellent example of hard-to-learn and hard-to-sample distribution, still accessible for mathematical analysis.

Our wavelet diffusion considers the sampling of the conditional probability p​(x¯1|x1)𝑝conditionalsubscript¯𝑥1subscript𝑥1p(\bar{x}_{1}|x_{1}) instead of p​(x0)𝑝subscript𝑥0p(x_{0}), by inverting the noise diffusion projected on the wavelet coefficients. Theorem 2 indicates that the loss obtained with any SGM-type method depends on the regularity parameters of ∇log⁡pt∇subscript𝑝𝑡\nabla\log p_{t} in (10).

Strictly speaking, to get a bound on K𝐾K we should control the norm of ∇2log⁡ptsuperscript∇2subscript𝑝𝑡\nabla^{2}\log p_{t} over all x𝑥x and t𝑡t. However, a look at the proof of the theorem indicates that this control does not have to be uniform in x𝑥x; for instance, there is no need to control this Hessian in domains which have an extremely small probability under ptsubscript𝑝𝑡p_{t}. Moreover, since ptsubscript𝑝𝑡p_{t} is a convolution between p0subscript𝑝0p_{0} and a Gaussian, we expect that a control over ∇2log⁡p0​(x)superscript∇2subscript𝑝0𝑥\nabla^{2}\log p_{0}(x) will actually be sufficient to control ∇2log⁡pt​(x)superscript∇2subscript𝑝𝑡𝑥\nabla^{2}\log p_{t}(x) for all t>0𝑡0t>0; these facts are non-rigorous for the moment. The distribution of some spectral statistics of ∇2log⁡p0superscript∇2subscript𝑝0\nabla^{2}\log p_{0} over samples drawn from the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4}-model are shown in Figure 6 (blue).

Considering conditional probabilities p¯¯𝑝\bar{p} instead of p𝑝p acts on the Hessian of the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4}-energy as a projection over the wavelet field: in the general context of (22),

−∇x2log⁡p​(x0)=K+∇2V​(x0),−∇x¯12log⁡p​(x¯1|x1)=γ2​G¯​(K+∇2V​(x0))​G¯⊤.formulae-sequencesubscriptsuperscript∇2𝑥𝑝subscript𝑥0𝐾superscript∇2𝑉subscript𝑥0subscriptsuperscript∇2subscript¯𝑥1𝑝conditionalsubscript¯𝑥1subscript𝑥1superscript𝛾2¯𝐺𝐾superscript∇2𝑉subscript𝑥0superscript¯𝐺top\displaystyle-\nabla^{2}_{x}\log p(x_{0})=K+\nabla^{2}V(x_{0}),\qquad-\nabla^{2}_{\bar{x}_{1}}\log p(\bar{x}_{1}|x_{1})=\gamma^{2}\bar{G}(K+\nabla^{2}V(x_{0}))\bar{G}^{\top}.(432)The proof is in Section 13. The distribution of the conditioning number of ∇x2log⁡psuperscriptsubscript∇𝑥2𝑝\nabla_{x}^{2}\log p and ∇x¯2log⁡psubscriptsuperscript∇2¯𝑥𝑝\nabla^{2}_{\bar{x}}\log p over samples drawn from the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} model is shown at Figure 6: the Hessian of the wavelet log-probability is orders-of-magnitude better conditioned than its single-scale counterpart, with a very concentrated distribution. The same phenomenon occurs at each scale j𝑗j, and the same is true for λmin,λmaxsubscript𝜆subscript𝜆\lambda_{\min},\lambda_{\max}. It turns out that considering wavelet coefficient not only concentrates these eigenvalues, but also drives λminsubscript𝜆\lambda_{\min} away from 0. In the context of multiscale Gaussian processes, Section 9 gives a rigorous proof of this phenomenon. In the general case, ∇2log⁡ptsuperscript∇2subscript𝑝𝑡\nabla^{2}\log p_{t} is not reduced to the inverse of a covariance matrix, but we expect the same phenomenon to be true.

In this section, we give some details on our numerical experiments from Section 4.1.

We used samples from the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} model generated using a classical MCMC algorithm — the sampling script will be publicly available in our repository.

The wavelet decompositions of our fields were performed using Python’s pywavelets package and Pytorch Wavelets package. For synthetic experiments, we used the Daubechies wavelets with p=4𝑝4p=4 vanishing moments (see (Mallat,, 1999, Section 7.2.3)).

At the first scale j=0𝑗0j=0, the distribution of the φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} models falls into the general form given in (22), and it is assumed that at each scale j𝑗j, the distribution of the field at scale j𝑗j still assumes this shape — with modified constants and coupling parameters. The score model we use at each scale is given by:sK,θ​(x)=12​x⊤​K​x+∑u(θ1​v1​(x​(u))+⋯+θm​vm​(x​(u))),subscript𝑠𝐾𝜃𝑥12superscript𝑥top𝐾𝑥subscript𝑢subscript𝜃1subscript𝑣1𝑥𝑢⋯subscript𝜃𝑚subscript𝑣𝑚𝑥𝑢\textstyle{{{s}}_{K,\theta}(x)=\frac{1}{2}x^{\top}Kx+\sum_{u}(\theta_{1}v_{1}(x(u))+\dotsb+\theta_{m}v_{m}(x(u))),}(433)where the parameters are K,θ1,…,θm𝐾subscript𝜃1…subscript𝜃𝑚K,\theta_{1},\dotsc,\theta_{m} and visubscript𝑣𝑖v_{i} are a family of smooth functions. One can also represent this score as sK,θ=K⋅x​x⊤+θ⊤​U​(x)subscript𝑠𝐾𝜃⋅𝐾𝑥superscript𝑥topsuperscript𝜃top𝑈𝑥s_{K,\theta}=K\cdot xx^{\top}+\theta^{\top}U(x) where Ui​(x)=∑uvi​(x​(u))subscript𝑈𝑖𝑥subscript𝑢subscript𝑣𝑖𝑥𝑢U_{i}(x)=\sum_{u}v_{i}(x(u)).

We trained our various algorithms using SGM or WSGM up to a time T=5𝑇5T=5 with ntrain=2000subscript𝑛train2000n_{\mathrm{train}}=2000 steps of forward diffusion. At each step t𝑡t, the parameters were learned by minimizing the score loss:ℓ​(K,θ)=𝔼​[|∇sK,θ​(xt)|2+2​Δx​sK,θ​(xt)]ℓ𝐾𝜃𝔼delimited-[]superscript∇subscript𝑠𝐾𝜃subscript𝑥𝑡22subscriptΔ𝑥subscript𝑠𝐾𝜃subscript𝑥𝑡\ell(K,\theta)=\mathbb{E}[|\nabla s_{K,\theta}(x_{t})|^{2}+2\Delta_{x}s_{K,\theta}(x_{t})]using the Adam optimiser with learning rate lr=0.01lr0.01\texttt{lr}=0.01 and default parameters α,β𝛼𝛽\alpha,\beta. At the start of the diffusion (t=0𝑡0t=0) we use 10000 steps of gradient descent. For t>1𝑡1t>1, we use only 100 steps of gradient descent, but initialized at (Kt−1,θt−1)subscript𝐾𝑡1subscript𝜃𝑡1(K_{t-1},\theta_{t-1}).

For the sampling, we used uniform steps of discretization.

For the error metric, we first measure the L2superscript𝐿2L^{2}-norm between the power spectra P,P^𝑃^𝑃P,\hat{P} of the true φ4superscript𝜑4\varphi^{4} samples, and our syntheses; more precisely, we set:D1=‖P−P^‖2.subscript𝐷1superscriptnorm𝑃^𝑃2D_{1}=\|P-\hat{P}\|^{2}.This error on second-order statistics is perfectly suitable for Gaussian processes, but must be refined for non-Gaussian processes. We also consider the total variation distance between the histograms of the marginal distributions (in the case of two-dimensions, pixel-wise histograms). We note this error D2subscript𝐷2D_{2}; our final error measure is D1+D2subscript𝐷1subscript𝐷2D_{1}+D_{2}. This is the error used in Figure Figure 6.

In the sequel, ∇f∇𝑓\nabla f is the gradient of a function f𝑓f, and ∇2superscript∇2\nabla^{2} is the Hessian matrix of f𝑓f. The Laplacian of f𝑓f is the trace of the Hessian.

Let U:ℝn→ℝ:𝑈→superscriptℝ𝑛ℝU:\mathbb{R}^{n}\to\mathbb{R} be smooth and M𝑀M be a n×m𝑛𝑚n\times m matrix. We set F​(x)=U​(M​x)𝐹𝑥𝑈𝑀𝑥F(x)=U(Mx) where x∈ℝm𝑥superscriptℝ𝑚x\in\mathbb{R}^{m}. Then, ∇2F​(x)=M⊤​∇2U​(x)​Msuperscript∇2𝐹𝑥superscript𝑀topsuperscript∇2𝑈𝑥𝑀\nabla^{2}F(x)=M^{\top}\nabla^{2}U(x)M.

For the second term, let us first recall that if x¯1subscript¯𝑥1\bar{x}_{1} and x1subscript𝑥1x_{1} are the wavelet coefficients and low-frequencies of x𝑥x, they are linked by (20). We use Haar wavelets. The 128×128128128128\times 128 original images are thus successively brought to the 64×64646464\times 64 and 32×32323232\times 32 resolutions, separately for each color channel. Each of the 333 channels of xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} and 999 channels of x¯jsubscript¯𝑥𝑗\bar{x}_{j} are normalized to have zero mean and unit variance.

Following Nichol and Dhariwal, (2021), both the conditional and unconditional scores are parametrized by a neural network with a UNet architecture. It has 333 residual blocks at each scale, with a base number of channels of C=128𝐶128C=128. The number of channels at the k𝑘k-th scale is ak​Csubscript𝑎𝑘𝐶a_{k}C, where the multipliers (ak)ksubscriptsubscript𝑎𝑘𝑘(a_{k})_{k} depend on the resolution of the generated images. They are (1,2,2,4,4)12244(1,2,2,4,4) for models at the 128×128128128128\times 128 resolution, (2,2,4,4)2244(2,2,4,4) for models at the 64×64646464\times 64 resolution, (4,4)44(4,4) for the conditional model at the 32×32323232\times 32 resolution, and (1,2,2,2)1222(1,2,2,2) for the unconditional model at the 32×32323232\times 32 resolution. All models include multi-head attention layers at in blocks operating on images at resolutions 16×16161616\times 16 and 8×8888\times 8. The conditioning on the low frequencies xjsubscript𝑥𝑗x_{j} is done with a simple input concatenation along channels, while conditioning on time is done through affine rescalings with learned time embeddings at each GroupNorm layer (Nichol and Dhariwal,, 2021; Saharia et al.,, 2021).

The networks are trained with the (conditional) denoising score matching losses:ℓ​(θJ)ℓsubscript𝜃𝐽\displaystyle\ell(\theta_{J})=𝔼xJ,t,z​[‖sθJ​(t,e−t​xJ+1−e−2​t​z)−z‖2]absentsubscript𝔼subscript𝑥𝐽𝑡𝑧delimited-[]superscriptnormsubscript𝑠subscript𝜃𝐽𝑡superscript𝑒𝑡subscript𝑥𝐽1superscript𝑒2𝑡𝑧𝑧2\displaystyle=\mathbb{E}_{x_{J},t,z}\left[||s_{\theta_{J}}(t,e^{-t}x_{J}+\sqrt{1-e^{-2t}}z)-z||^{2}\right](438)ℓ​(θ¯j)ℓsubscript¯𝜃𝑗\displaystyle\ell(\bar{\theta}_{j})=𝔼x¯j,xj,t,z[||s¯θ¯j(t,e−tx¯j+1−e−2​tz|xj)−z||2]\displaystyle=\mathbb{E}_{\bar{x}_{j},x_{j},t,z}\left[||\bar{s}_{\bar{\theta}_{j}}(t,e^{-t}\bar{x}_{j}+\sqrt{1-e^{-2t}}z\,|\,x_{j})-z||^{2}\right](439)where z∼𝒩​(0,Id)similar-to𝑧𝒩0Idz\sim\mathcal{N}(0,\operatorname{Id}) and the time t𝑡t is distributed as T​u2𝑇superscript𝑢2Tu^{2}with u∼𝒰​([0,1])similar-to𝑢𝒰01u\sim\mathcal{U}([0,1]). We fix the maximum time T=5𝑇5T=5 for allscales. Networks are trained for 5×1055superscript1055\times{10}^{5} gradient steps with a batchsize of 128128128 at the 32×32323232\times 32 resolution and 646464 otherwise. We use theAdam optimizer with a learning rate of 10−4superscript104{10}^{-4} and no weight decay.

For sampling, we use model parameters from an exponential moving average with a rate of 0.99990.99990.9999. For each number of discretization steps N𝑁N, we use the Euler-Maruyama discretization with a uniform step size δk=T/Nsubscript𝛿𝑘𝑇𝑁\delta_{k}=T/N starting from T=5𝑇5T=5. This discretization scheme is used at all scales. For FID computations, we generate 30,0003000030,000 samples in each setting.
